These are the only currently established occurrences of any siricomorph family in the Ypresian. We treat the undescribed new siricoid from the Cretaceous Crato Formation of Brazil as belonging to the Pseudosiricidae, not Siricidae, and agree with various authors that the Ypresian<jats:italic>Megapterites mirabilis</jats:italic>Cockerell is an ant (Hymenoptera: Formicidae). The Miocene species<jats:italic>Cephites oeningensis</jats:italic>Heer and<jats:italic>C. fragilis</jats:italic>Heer, assigned to the Cephidae over a century and a half ago, are also ants. Many of the host plants that siricomporphs feed upon today first appeared in the Eocene, a number of these in the Okanagan Highlands in particular. The Okanagan Highlands sites where these wasps were found also had upper microthermal mean annual temperatures as are overwhelmingly preferred by most modern siricomorphs, but were uncommon in the globally warm Ypresian, only found then in higher elevations and highest latitudes.</jats:p>", "DOI": "10.4039/tce.2015.55", "type": "journal-article", "created": {"date-parts": [[2015, 10, 27]], "date-time": "2015-10-27T10:32:31Z", "timestamp": 1445941951000}, "page": "209-228", "source": "Crossref", "is-referenced-by-count": 4, "title": ["New early Eocene Siricomorpha (Hymenoptera: Symphyta: Pamphiliidae, Siricidae, Cephidae) from the Okanagan Highlands, western North America"], "prefix": "10.4039", "volume": "148", "author": [{"given": "S.", "family": "Bruce Archibald", "sequence": "first", "affiliation": []}, {"given": "Alexandr P.", "family": "Rasnitsyn", "sequence": "additional", "affiliation": []}], "member": "56", "published-online": {"date-parts": [[2015, 10, 27]]}, "reference": [{"key": "S0008347X15000553_ref73", "first-page": "1", "article-title": "The European Pamphiliidae (Hymenoptera, Symphyta), with special reference to the Netherlands. "issue": "1", "license": [{"URL": "https://www.cambridge.org/core/terms", "start": {"date-parts": [[2016, 5, 20]], "date-time": "2016-05-20T00:00:00Z", "timestamp": 1463702400000}, "delay-in-days": Paleontol."], "published-print": {"date-parts": [[2013, 1]]}, "abstract": "<jats:p>The early Eocene green lacewings (Neuroptera, Chrysopidae) of the Okanagan Highlands deposits of McAbee, and Driftwood Canyon, British Columbia (Canada) and Republic, Washington (U.S.A.) are treated in detail for the first time. At least six genera are present, one unnamed, three new, with at least 10 new species, six named:<jats:italic>Protochrysa fuscobasalis</jats:italic>n. sp. (McAbee) (Limaiinae, the youngest known record of the subfamily),<jats:italic>Okanaganochrysa coltsunae</jats:italic>n. gen. n. sp. (McAbee),<jats:italic>Adamsochrysa aspera</jats:italic>n. gen. n. sp. (McAbee),<jats:italic>A. wilsoni</jats:italic>n. gen. n. sp. (Republic),<jats:italic>Archaeochrysa profracta</jats:italic>n. sp. (McAbee), and<jats:italic>Pseudochrysopa harveyi</jats:italic>n. gen. n. sp. (Driftwood Canyon) (all Nothochrysinae, the latter provisionally). The four unnamed species include one assigned to<jats:italic>Pseudochrysopa</jats:italic>, two likely belonging to<jats:italic>Adamsochrysa</jats:italic>, and one of an unknown nothochrysine genus. Microtholi are detected on the abdominal sclerites of<jats:italic>Adamsochrysa wilsoni</jats:italic>, and the spermatheca and spermathecal duct in the abdomen of<jats:italic>Pseudochrysopa harveyi</jats:italic>, the first reported occurrences of these preserved in fossil Chrysopidae. Structures were detected on the apical wing margins of some species that appear similar to trichosors, which are unknown in Chrysopidae, but are present in some other neuropteran families. This is the richest described assemblage of the family anywhere in the fossil record. Okanagan chrysopids were also morphologically and presumably ecologically diverse, including large species with rich venation and well as those with simplified venation and the smallest known fossil species. Pipunculinae species A is the oldest known member of the family whose wing lacks a separated M<jats:sub>2</jats:sub> vein; previously this had been known in species only as old as Miocene Dominican amber. This is a presumably derived character state that is predominant in modern species. Molecular analysis indicates an origin of the Pipunculidae in the Maastrichtian; the morphological and taxonomic diversity seen here in the Ypresian is consistent with an early radiation of the family. ["Can Entomol"], "published-print": {"date-parts": [[2018, 4]]}, "abstract": "<jats:title>Abstract</jats:title><jats:p>Most major modern families of Hymenoptera were established in the Mesozoic, but the diversifications within ecologically key trophic guilds and lineages that significantly influence the character of modern terrestrial ecosystems \u2013 bees (Apiformes), ants (Formicidae), social Vespidae, parasitoids (Ichneumonidae), and phytophagous Tenthredinoidea \u2013 were previously known to occur mostly in the middle to late Eocene. We find these changes earlier, seen here in the early Eocene Okanagan Highlands fossil deposits of western North America. Some of these may have occurred even earlier, but have been obscured by taphonomic processes. We provide an overview of the Okanagan Highlands Hymenoptera to family level and in some cases below that, with a minimum of 25 named families and at least 30 when those tentatively assigned or distinct at family level, but not named are included. Some are poorly known as fossils (Trigonalidae, Siricidae, Peradeniidae, Monomachidae), and some represent the oldest confirmed occurrences (Trigonalidae, Pompilidae, Sphecidae<jats:italic>sensu stricto</jats:italic>, Peradeniidae, Monomachidae, and possibly Halictidae). Some taxa previously thought to be relictual or extinct by the end of the Cretaceous (Angarosphecidae, Archaeoscoliinae, some Diapriidae) are present and sometimes abundant in the early Eocene. Living relatives of some taxa are now present in different climate regimes or on different continents.</jats:p>", "DOI": "10.4039/tce.2017.59", "type": "journal-article", "created": {"date-parts": [[2018, 1, 8]], "date-time": "2018-01-08T09:13:48Z", "timestamp": 1515402828000}, "page": "205-257", "source": "Crossref", "is-referenced-by-count": 12, "title": ["Modernisation of the Hymenoptera: ants, bees, wasps, and sawflies of the early Eocene Okanagan Highlands of western North America"], "prefix": "10.4039", "volume": "150", "author": [{"given": "S. Entomol. Res."], "published-print": {"date-parts": [[1943, 4]]}, "abstract": "<jats:p>1. These studies were originally intended to form the basis of a world monograph of the S<jats:sc>iricidae</jats:sc> ; such a work cannot be completed under present circumstances.</jats:p><jats:p>2. Two ratios are introduced as useful characters for separating species : the ovipositor/forewing ratio and the sawsheath/ovipositor ratio. These ratios were obtained from all specimens of all the species represented in the British Museum collections and the results are tabulated. They were found not to vary with the size of the insects.</jats:p><jats:p>The former ratio is specially useful in the genus <jats:italic>Urocerus</jats:italic>, which has a long ovipositor, and the latter ratio in the genus <jats:italic>Sirex</jats:italic>, which has a shorter ovipositor.</jats:p><jats:p>3. Keys are given to the genera of the world. Of Semenov's new genera, <jats:italic>Xoanon</jats:italic> is accepted but not <jats:italic>Xanthosirex</jats:italic>. A new genus <jats:italic>Eriotremex</jats:italic> is erected for certain Indo-Malayan species previously included in <jats:italic>Tremex</jats:italic>.</jats:p><jats:p>4. Keys are given to the European species, which are compared critically with related species from other parts of the world. A key to the species of <jats:italic>Eriotremex</jats:italic>, gen. nov., is also given.</jats:p><jats:p>5. <jats:italic>Sirex noctilio</jats:italic>, F., and <jats:italic>S. juvencus</jats:italic>, L., are recorded for the first time from North America and <jats:italic>S. cyaneus</jats:italic>, F., from the continent of Europe. The common <jats:italic>Urocerus</jats:italic> of the northern Palaearctic region is shown to be more closely related to the Nearctic <jats:italic>U. gigas flavicornis</jats:italic>, F., than to the central European <jats:italic>U. gigas gigas</jats:italic>, L., and is treated as a new subspecies\u2014<jats:italic>U. gigas taiganus</jats:italic>, subsp. nov. <jats:italic>U. gigas tibetanus</jats:italic>, subsp. nov., is described from the Himalayas. <jats:italic>U. sah</jats:italic>, Mocs\u00e1ry, is treated as a subspecies of <jats:italic>U. augur</jats:italic>, Klug, and <jats:italic>U. cedrorum</jats:italic>, Smith, as a synonym of <jats:italic>U. augur augur</jats:italic>, Klug.</jats:p><jats:p>6. The British S<jats:sc>iricidae</jats:sc> are discussed, and it is suggested that <jats:italic>U. gigas taiganus</jats:italic>, subsp. nov., and the form of <jats:italic>S. juvencus</jats:italic>, L., with entirely black antennae may be native in the Caledonian forest.</jats:p><jats:p>7. It is argued that modern S<jats:sc>iricidae</jats:sc> could not have been derived from the Jurassic P<jats:sc>seudosiricidae</jats:sc>.</jats:p><jats:p>8. The known Oriental and Himalayan S<jats:sc>iricidae</jats:sc> are listed and discussed. <jats:italic>Urocerus multifasciatus</jats:italic>, Takeuchi, and <jats:italic>Eriotremex formosanus</jats:italic>, Matsumura, are mentioned as two species originally described from Formosa but shown also to occur on the mainland. <jats:italic>Urocerus niger</jats:italic>, sp. nov., is described from the Himalayan region, and the name <jats:italic>Eriotremex malayanus</jats:italic>, sp. nov., is given to a form described without a name by Forsius from Malaya.</jats:p><jats:p>9.
